1.
SUMMARY
The  Dieter  Lake  Property  consists  of  496  mineral  titles  totalling  approximately  23,836 
hectares, located about 150 km north of Hydro-Quebec Reservoir LG-4.  It is accessible with a 
helicopter or fixed-wing aircraft.  It is held 100% by Strathmore Minerals Corp.
Uranium mineralization at Dieter Lake occurs within a Lower Proterozoic sedimentary basin, 
within the Superior Structural Province of the Precambrian Shield.  Between Hudson Bay and 
Labrador Trough, north-central Quebec, are two east-west trending belts of sedimentary outliers 
attributed  to  the  Sakami  Formation.    The  basins  and  grabens  are  remnants  of  a  failed 
intracratonic  rift  system.    The  Sakami  Formation  rests  unconformably  on  Archean  granite, 
diorite,  migmatite,  gneiss,  amphibolites,  metavolcanics  and  schists  cut  by  late  diorite  and 
pegmatitic dykes.  The Sakami Formation exceeds 1000 m thickness and is divisible into a lower 
continental  sequence  of  lacustrine  and  fluvial-deltaic  sediments,  and  an  upper  continental 
arenite sequence.  The Gayot Lake outlier, which is host to the uranium mineralization at Dieter 
Lake, measures approximately 52 km east-west, by 12 km north-south.
Suggested  deposit  types  for  the  uranium  mineralization  at  Dieter  Lake  have  included
unconformity-type, black shale type, and syngenetic stratabound.
Uranium mineralization at the property is in the form of fine-grained, sooty pitchblende within 
a shale-wacke horizon of the Sakami Formation.  The pitchblende is accompanied by various 
sulphides and moderately associated with metallic elements Fe, Cu, V and Mo.  Molybdenum 
grades of up to 0.8% over 1 m and 0.22% over 3 m were encountered in the Lake Vivian area.  
The  uranium  ore  horizon  bed  has  been  traced  over  an  east-west  distance  of  5  km  and  is 
generally 20 to 80 m above the unconformity.  It ranges from 0.2 to 3 m thick, and has been 
observed up to 5 m thick.  To the west it is generally green and gradually changes color to red in 
the  east,  representing  a  transition  from  euxenic  to  oxidizing  conditions.    The  best  grades  of 
mineralization  are  reported  to  occur  with  the  transitional  facies  along  the  slope  of  basement 
highs.
Uranerz  Exploration  and  Mining  conducted  significant  exploration  in  the  late  1970’s  and 
early  1980’s.    Extensive  mapping  and  sampling  programs  were  completed,  involving  the 
collection  of  rock,  soil,  lake  water,  and  lake  sediment  samples.    Airborne  and  ground 
geophysical programs were completed; as well as, diamond drilling, including at least 145 holes.
2
At least four main areas of mineralization have been documented, from west to east: Vivian, 
Nancy I, Nancy II, and Bert’s.  For these and other areas, historical resource estimates have 
been published, as summarized in the section entitled “Mineralization”.
Strathmore Minerals retained Mr. James A. McCrea, P. Geo., during September of 2004 to 
complete  an  independent  review  of  the  Dieter  Lake  Property.  Mr.  James  McCrea,  a  qualified 
person under NI 43-101, visited the Dieter Lake Property and surrounding area on 02 October 
2004.  A report entitled “Technical Report on the Dieter Lake Uranium Property, North Central 
Quebec;  NI  43-101  Report  for  Strathmore  Minerals  Corp”  was  subsequently  written  06  May 
2005.
A  comprehensive  exploration  program  was  carried  out  during  the  summer  of  2005,  at  the 
Dieter  Lake  Property.    Work  was  based  out  of  a  camp  on  Dieter  Lake  and  consisted  of 
rehabilitating old core racks, relogging and sampling core, flag gridding, geochemical sampling, 
regional geologic mapping and a GEOTEM® airborne survey.
This  work  was  planned  and  carried  out  by  Dahrouge  Geological  Consulting  Ltd.  of 
Edmonton, Alberta.  A total of approximately 158.6 line km were laid in a 200 m by 200 m grid.  
Geochemical sampling consisted of 834 sediment samples, most of which were collected on the 
grid approximately every 200 m.  During regional geologic and structural mapping of the Gayot 
Lake Basin, 36 hand samples were collected from outcrops and boulders.  This work resulted in 
an updated structural and geologic map of the basin including north trending gabbroic dykes.  It 
also  led  to  the  extension  the  claimed  area  to  the  northwest  of  the  original  property  and  the 
eastern  margin  of  the  basin,  where  anomalous  scintillometer  readings  were  recorded  in 
tectonized  sediments.    Old  core  found  at  Dieter  and  Ranger  lakes  was  relogged,  and  204 
samples were taken based on scintillometer readings.  Results of the core sampling were used 
to verify historic data and to develop an updated resource model.
Racks  and  boxes  were  rehabilitated  where  possible.    The  Fugro  GEOTEM®  survey, 
including magnetic data, covered 977 line km over the Gayot Lake Basin.  The survey consisted 
of 29 east-west lines, between 8 and 43 km long, with 400m line spacing.  This survey resulted 
in extension of claimed area to include an electromagnetic anomaly onto the northeast of the 
original property.
A  new  resource  estimate  was  completed  using  both  2005  sample  data  and  historic  data.  
This  estimate,  which  used  200  m  radius  polygons  and  a  minimum  cutoff  of  200  ppm  over  1 
metre,  resulted  in  an  Inferred  Resource  of  24.4  million  lbs  U3O8,  with  an  average  grade  of
3
0.057%.  The Dieter Lake Property is a property of merit sufficient to warrant further exploration.  
Initial  recommendations  involve  further  ground  exploration  to  follow  up  on  areas  of  interest 
outlined by the historic work and the 2005 exploration program.    A proposed diamond drilling 
program  will  include  a  minimum  10  to  12  holes,  totalling  approximately  5000  metres,  within 
mineralized areas defined in the past, as well as in unexplored areas of interest.
2.
INTRODUCTION AND TERMS OF REFERENCE
2.1
Terms of Reference
This technical report on the Dieter Lake Property, was prepared at the request of Mr. David 
Miller, President and Chief Operating Officer of Strathmore Minerals Corporation (Strathmore).  
It has been prepared to comply with the standards outlined in National Instrument 43-101 for the 
Canadian Securities Administration.
The  information  upon  which  this  technical  report  is  based  was  obtained  from  both  public
records, and from field evaluations conducted by the author during the summer of 2005.
An exploration program was carried out on the Dieter Lake property from late June to late 
August  2005  by  Dahrouge  Geological  Consulting  Ltd  (Dahrouge).    This  assessment  report 
describes  the  exploration  conducted  on  the  property,  which  is  located  about  150  km  north  of 
Hydro-Quebec Reservoir LG-4.  This report includes information on the samples collected, core 
logging,  geologic  interpretations  and  airborne  geophysical  survey  results.    This  work  was 
authorized  by  Devinder  Randhawa,  President  and  CEO  of  Strathmore  Minerals  Corp 
(Strathmore) at the time the work was conducted.
Mr. Clinton Davis, a qualified person under NI 43-101, supervised work at the Dieter Lake
Property and surrounding area from 22 June to 20 August, 2005.
2.2   Sources of Information and Disclaimer
This  technical  report  is  based  upon  published  and  unpublished  data,  primarily  from 
geological reports as described in the sections herein entitled History and References.  Most of 
these  reports  were  written  prior  to  the  implementation  of  the  standards  relating  to  National 
Instrument 43-101.  However, as persons holding post-secondary degrees in geology or related 
fields prepared the reports, the reports and relevant data are considered to be of high-quality.  
Additional information was obtained while working the property, by the author.
The  author  does  not  take  responsibility  for  the  accuracy  of  the  historical  data  described
herein.
3.
GEOGRAPHIC SETTING
3.1
Property Location and Description
4
The property is centred on latitude 55°58'N, longitude 70°34'W (Fig. 3.1). During the fall of 
2004,  the  Dieter  Lake  Property  encompassed  168  claims  totalling  8,106  hectares  in  north-
central Quebec.  An additional 48 claims, adjoining to the south, totalling 4,512 hectares, were 
staked in March 2005.  During and up to late summer, 236 more claims were staked, for a total 
area  for  the  property  of  21,809  hectares  and  452  claims,  (Fig.  3.2,  and  Table  3.1).    During 
March of 2006, an additional 45 claims (2074 ha) were designated, and were under application 
at the time of writing.

3.2
Access and Local Resources
5
Currently, the area can only be accessed by helicopter or fixed-wing aircraft; however, an 
all-weather road to the S.E.B.J. base at Lac Pau-Duplanter comes within 140 km of the project 
area (Holmstead, 1983).  As well, Caniapiscau is approximately 120 km straight-line from Dieter 
Lake. During summer months, access to, and within, the property area is either by float plane or 
helicopter, with limited quad access.  During winter months, access is by ski equipped aircraft, 
helicopter or snowmobile.
Accommodations are available at Mirage Outfitters, which is located about 10 km east of the 
Hydro-Quebec Reservoir LG-4.  Mirage Outfitters is approximately 1½ hours via aircraft from the 
property and provides all of the necessary supplies and services, including power, fuel, water 
and food.  An airstrip is available at the Hydro-Quebec Reservoir LG-4.  Mirage is accessible via 
aircraft  or  along  the  Transtaiga  Road  from  Radisson.    Mirage  Outfitters  is  1,575  km  from 
Quebec  City  along  the  Transtaiga  Road.    Dieter  Lake  is  a  2-3  hour  single  Otter  flight  from 
Radisson/LaGrande, depending on the prevailing winds.  A camp was set up in May 2005 on 
Dieter Lake.
There is an abandoned camp on the south shore of Ranger Lake.  It is in poor condition and 
would require upgrades and material removal prior to use.   A large number of lakes on and near 
the  property  provide  adequate  supplies  of  water  for  drinking,  travel,  and  future  drilling  and 
mining operations.
3.3
Topography and Vegetation
The climate of the area is subarctic-continental with temperatures reaching lows of -50°C in 
the winter and highs of 30°C in the summer.  Lake freezing commonly occurs in early-October 
and ice break-up generally occurs in mid- to late-May.  Winds are common and often reach 40 
mph.  Mean rainfall during the period of May to September is 450 mm.
The topography of the area generally consists of gently rolling hills with elevations of about 
450 to 550 metres above sea level.  Glacio-fluvial deposits constitute many of the topographic 
features.  Other glacial features, such as moraines, erratics and eskers, are common throughout 
the area.  Drainage of major rivers is west towards Hudson Bay and north towards Ungava Bay.
The area lies in a transitional vegetation zone between subarctic and tundra.  The result is 
stunted open forest cover, dominated by black spruce and tamarack, along the shores of lakes 
and rivers, and tundra barrens found on the highland.

The  Dieter  Lake  Property  has  a  documented  history  of  exploration  of  about  30  years, 
starting  in  1961  with  by  the  Geological  Survey  of  Canada.    Exploration  of  this  region  is 
considered sporadic.  The majority of significant activity occurring in the late 1970's and early
1980's, supervised by Uranerz Exploration and Mining Ltd in joint venture with the Société de 
Développement de la Baie James.  
5.
GEOLOGIC SETTING
7
5.1
Regional Geology
Sedimentary rocks of the Sakami Formation appear as a series of outliers, comprising two 
ENE-WSW  trending  discontinuous  belts  within  the  eastern  arm  of  the  Superior  Structural 
Province  of  the  Canadian  Shield  in  northern  Quebec,  (Fig.  5.1).    The  two  belts  are 
approximately 200 km apart and extend for about 300 km in length, between Hudson Bay and 
Labrador  Trough.    The  belts  appear  representative  of  an  early  intracontinental  rift  system, 
containing  several  basins/grabens  within  Archean  basement  rocks.    The  graben  structures 
allowed  for  the  deposition  and  preservation  of  Sakami  Formation  sediments.    The  Sakami 
Formation  underwent  block  faulting  during  and  following  deposition,  as  the  graben  controlling 
faults remained active (Orr, 1978).  The only other tectonic activity evident in the Sakami rocks 
is slight tilting to the south.
Based  on  depositional  environments,  the  Geological  Survey  of  Canada  (Eade,  1966) 
subdivided  the  Sakami  Formation  into  lower  and  upper  members.    The  lower  unit  is  a 
continental  sequence  with  lacustrine  and  basal  fluvial-deltaic  sediments,  and  is  up  to  370  m 
thick.  It consists of a series of interbedded arkosic sandstones, red and green shales, siltstones 
and  wackes  with  minor  conglomeratic  beds.    It  displays  general  coarsening  upwards  of 
sediments,  rapid  lateral  facies  variations,  and  gradational  contacts  between  lithologies.    The 
upper  unit  exceeds  1000  m  thickness  and  conformably  overlies  the  lower  unit.    It  consists  of 
massive  1-2  m  beds  of  clean,  homogenous  quartz  arenites.    The  unit  is  an  epicontinental 
sequence  and  displays  well  developed,  planar-type  cross  bedding.    A  general  paleocurrent 
orientation is to the south and east with a likely sediment source to the north and northwest (Orr, 
1979).  Undisturbed Sakami beds display a general east-west attitude with southerly dips of 10° 
to 30°.
Uranium mineralization has been discovered in sediments of the Sakami Formation located 
near the Proterozoic/Archean unconformity.   Age dating by Uranerz Exploration and Mining Ltd. 
on pitchblende from lower Sakami shales determined a Pb-Pb age of 1860 Ma, indicating an 
early  Proterozoic,  late  Aphebian  age.    The  age  assumes  coeval  deposition  of  uranium  and 
Sakami sediments (Orr, 1979).
8
The  Sakami  sediments  unconformably  overly  Archean  rocks.    Either  at  a  fault  or 
nonconformable  contact.    The  Archean  basement  rocks  consist  of  granite,  diorite,  migmatite, 
para- and orthogneisses, amphibolite, metavolcanics, and minor schists cut by pegmatite and 
diorite  dykes  (Dahlkamp,  2004a;  Orr,  1979).    Deformation  of  the  basement  rocks,  including 
folding  and  faulting,  intrusion,  and  metamorphism  (greenschist  to  amphibolitic  and  locally 
granulite  facies)  occurred  during  the  Kenoran  Orogeny  approximately  2480  million  years  ago 
(Petura and Plouffe, 1981).
5.2
Property Geology
In general, the regional geology summarizes the geology of the Dieter Lake Property (Fig. 
5.1).  Of the two discontinuous erosional belts of Sakami sediments, the northernmost is the belt 
of interest at Dieter Lake.  Two outliers of the Sakami Formation are located at Gayot Lake and 
the Little Whale River-Mildred Lake area.  The entire uranium-hosting Gayot Lake Basin has an 
approximate east-west length of 52 km and north-south length of 12 km (Dahlkamp, 2004a).
The  majority  of  the  infilling  Sakami  sediments  in  the  Dieter  Lake  area  are  relatively 
indifferent  from  the  regional  occurrences;  however,  a  200  m  thick  transitional  zone  has  been 
recognized between the lower and upper Sakami formations, consisting of argillites to siltstones 
in  the  upper  and  lower  thirds,  with  quartz  arenite  in  the  middle.    The  transitional  zone  is 
arbitrarily  included  in  the  Lower  Sakami  Formation  (Gehrisch,  1981).    The  Sakami  sediments 
unconformably  overlie  Archean  basement  to  the  north  and  dip  gently  to  the  south  (15-20°).   
Faulting  has  influenced  deposition,  and  the  deformation  of  the  sediments  during  and  after 
sedimentation, causing major displacements and drag folding (Dahlkamp, 2004a).  Major faults 
trend approximately 35°.  Horst and graben structures exist as a result of a main fault system, 
which strikes at approximately 57° with a near vertical dip (Orr and Holmstead, 1980).
Well developed stratigraphic sections of Lower Sakami sediments are present in some areas 
at  Dieter  Lake,  including  thin  interbeds  of  quartz  pebble  conglomerates,  siltstones,  red  and 
green  wackes/shales  and  various  sandstones,  all  overlain  by  clean,  white  to  pink  quartz 
arenites, pink to green sandstones, and red and green shales of the Upper Sakami (Dahlkamp, 
2004a; Orr and Holmstead, 1980).  Conglomerate is the basal unit and is generally 2 to 5 m 
thick, consisting of cobbles and boulders of granitic material.  Above the conglomerate is a 5 to 
8  m,  medium-grained,  basal  sandstone.    The  conglomerate  and  sandstone  are  confined  to 
depressions in the basement controlled by the northeast-southwest trending faults.
A gap above the sandstone is interpreted from angular boulders of green siltstone observed 
within  the  till.    In  surrounding  areas  the  unit  is  observed  as  dominantly  green,  thin-bedded
mudstones with laminae of carbonate, siltstone, and sandy mudstone.  The laminated mudstone 
unit ranges from 9 to 80 m thick and contains very thin rhythmical laminae from less than 1 mm 
to 5 mm (Dahlkamp, 2004a).
9
The  overlying  unit  is  interpreted  as  a  turbidite  sequence  containing  sandy  to  arkosic 
mudstones,  sandstones  and  conglomerates.    It  consists  of  a  lower  subunit  of  mainly  green, 
laminated wacke and an upper subunit of oxidized, mainly red wacke.  The lower wackes exhibit 
a  slight  reddish  tinge  immediately  above  the  ore  horizon  in  areas  of  well  developed  uranium 
mineralization.    The  suggested  depositional  conditions  for  the  wackes  are  in  a  lacustrine 
environment by agitated water under reducing conditions.  Above the wackes are red and green 
alternating mudstones and siltstones, and pinkish- or greenish-white, fine-grained sandstones.  
The  unit  is  similar  to  the  underlying  turbidites  but  the  grain  sizes  are  generally  coarser  and 
quartz content is more abundant (Dahlkamp, 2004a).       
6.
DEPOSIT TYPE
The dominant mineral deposit type at the Dieter Lake Property is debatable.  Currently, the 
mineralized  horizon  appears  to  constitute  a  singular  stratabound  uranium  deposit.    Based  on 
Dahlkamp’s  (1993)  classification  scheme,  two  main  deposit  types  have  been  suggested, 
unconformity-contact  type  and  black  shale  type,  (McCrea,  2005).    Dieter  Lake  contains 
favourable and contrasting factors to both types; however, the property may constitute a unique 
style of geology and mineralization.
Unconformity-contact
type  deposits  are  associated  with  mineralization
that  occurs 
immediately  above  and  below  an  unconformable  contact  that  generally  separates  crystalline 
basement from overlying Proterozoic or Phanerozoic aged sediments.  Subtypes are based on 
the age of overlying sediments, Proterozoic subtype and Phanerozoic subtype.  There are two 
classes of Proterozoic unconformity subtype deposits; fracture-bound in altered metasediments 
immediately below an unconformity, and clay-bound directly above an unconformity (Dahlkamp, 
1993).  Uranium is most commonly found in the form of pitchblende or uraninite in both classes. 
In  fracture-bound  deposits,  uranium  is  generally  disseminated  to  massive,  in  structures 
within  Lower  Proterozoic  metasediments  containing  graphitic  horizons.    The  concentrated 
uranium mineralization is generally less than 200 metres below the unconformity.  The deposits 
commonly consist of medium to large reserves of medium grade.
Clay-bound  deposits  generally  contain  uranium  in  a  disseminated  to  massive  nature,  in 
tabular to lenticular horizontal ore bodies.  Uranium mineralization is hosted by clay and argillic
10
sandstone of pedogenic and/or early Athabasca diagenetic origin, overlying Lower Proterozoic 
metasediments.  The deposits are generally very high grade and contain large reserves.
An  example  of  fracture-bound  unconformity-contact  type  deposits  includes  Rabbit  Lake 
within  the  Athabasca  Basin,  northern  Saskatchewan.    The  operator  of  Rabbit  Lake,  Cameco 
Corporation  (Cameco)  has  reported  reserves  of  444,000  tonnes  with  grades  of  1.29%  U3O8 
(Cameco website).
Examples  of  clay-bound  unconformity-contact  type  deposits  include  Cigar  Lake  and  Key 
Lake,  both  operated  by  Cameco  within  the  Athabasca  Basin,  northern  Saskatchewan.    Cigar 
Lake is the world’s largest undeveloped high-grade uranium deposit, with total reported proven 
and probable reserves of 551,000 tonnes with a grade of 19.06% U3O8, and a capacity of 231.5 
million pounds of U3O8.  Key Lake is currently the world’s largest running high-grade uranium 
milling operation.  Reserves total 61,900 tonnes of 0.52% U3O8 (Cameco website).
Although  some  characteristics  of  the  Dieter  Lake  Deposit  are  similar  to  the  unconformity-
hosted  deposits  of  the  Saskatchewan  Athabasca  Basin  region,  several  factors  are  lacking.  
Dahlkamp (2004, p. 2) summarizes some of these factors:
layers  within  Aphebian  metasediments
“-No  intense  and  deep  reaching  lateritic  weathering  (regolithization)  of  the  crystalline 
basement prior to Sakami sedimentation; 
-No  redbed  sandstone  sequence  immediately  on  top  of  the  basement  and,  hence, 
diagenetic processes that are thought to have been involved in the formation of Athabasca 
unconformity deposits could not occur; 
-No  significant  tectonic-magmatic  events  are  known  in  this  part  of  the  Canadian  Shield, 
such as the Hudsonian Orogeny in northern Saskatchewan, to the final stage of which vein-
type U deposits are assumed to be related. 
…the described geological-lithological situation of the Dieter Lake U mineralization…may be 
compared  with  uraniferous 
in  northern 
Saskatchewan…prior to regional metamorphism and magmatism.” 
 
Black Shale type deposits are found within very fine grained, marine organic-rich shales or 
coal-rich  pyritic  shale.    The  black  shales  are  commonly  interbedded  with  sedimentary  rocks, 
such as limestone, sandstone, siltstone and shale beds.  Mineralization is generally uniformly 
disseminated  and  adsorbed  on  organic  material  and  clay  minerals,  within  synsedimentary 
stratiform  (Dahlkamp,  1993).    Associated  elements  may  include  small  quantities  of  copper, 
chromium, molybdenum, manganese, rare earth elements, vanadium and phosphorous.  Shale 
thickness is fairly uniform, usually a few metres and up to 10 m (Dahlkamp, 1993). The deposits 
commonly contain medium to large resources of low to medium grade.  Diagenetic processes 
are common, which can lead to concentrated or upgraded uranium enrichments.
11
Dahlkamp  (1993,  p.  132)  describes  likely  depositional  conditions  for  a  Black  Shale  type
uranium deposit:
“Black shales of marine origin were deposited in shallow, partially closed epicontinental 
basins within continental terrane tectonically stable for a long period.  The depositional 
environment is characterized by a low rate of sedimentation, brackish to normal marine 
salinities,  anaerobic,  strongly  reducing  conditions,  formation  of  saporpelic-bituminous, 
and  humic,  coaly  matter  from  planktonic  marine  algae  and  land  plant  (wood  spores) 
debris. 
Synsedimentary  deposition  of  U  from  seawater  by  adsorption  dominantly  on  organic 
matter with particular concentrations in humic-coaly material.  If phosphate nodules are 
present, they normally collected more uranium than the surrounding shale.” 
Gehrisch (1987) compares the Gayot Lake area, which includes the Dieter Lake Property, to 
the  Ranstad  uranium  deposit  in  central  southern  Sweden.    The  Ranstad  deposit  has  been 
defined  as  a  Black  Shale  type  uranium  deposit  by  Dahlkamp’s  (1993)  classification  scheme. 
Depositional processes evident in the Gayot Lake area are summarized by Gehrisch (1987, p. 
57);
“Conditions conclusive to syngenetic uranium mineralizations in sedimentary black shale 
to playa-type environments existed since the earth’s atmosphere attained a certain level 
of free oxygen.  The late Aphebian Gayot Lake uranium occurrence which is assumed to 
be  about  2000  ma  old  (Robbins  1980  and  Seguin  et  al.  1981),  represents  an  early 
example  of  chemical  uranium  mobilisation  and  transportation  by  oxidizing  meteoric 
waters and synsedimentary precipitation at redox boundaries.” 
Uranium mineralization at the Ranstad deposit is hosted in a kolm horizon (2.5 to 4 metres) 
within black bituminous alum shale.  The shale averages 15 m in thickness and extends laterally 
about  500  km2.    A  total  of  215  tonnes  of  uranium  oxide  with  an  ore  grade  of  0.03%  was 
extracted from mined shales in an open pit from 1965 to 1969.  Based on an average grade of 
200 to 300 ppm U3O8, the deposit contains an estimated 300,000 mt U3O8 (Dahlkamp, 1993).
Relating the Dieter Lake mineralization to that of the Ranstad deposit is not an accurate 
comparison due to contrasting factors.  The mineralization at Ranstad is contained within a kolm 
horizon with a much higher organic content (22%) than is found at Dieter Lake (<1%).  Uranium 
at Ranstad is adsorbed on kolm nodules that contain up to 80% organics (Dahlkamp, 2004b).  
The Dieter Lake deposit lacks this organic component.  This contrasting factor can be explained 
away because the Ranstad deposit is of Cambrian age, (Dahlkamp, 1993), while the Dieter Lake 
sediments are of Proterozoic age, before the explosion of life.  Thus, the Dieter Lake deposit 
could fit the black shale model, minus the carbonaceous material.
Uranerz Exploration and Mining Ltd. (UEM) suggested three possible models for the uranium 
deposition  discovered  at  Dieter  Lake:  disseminated  stratabound,  structurally  controlled
12
epigenetic, and an unconformity type uranium deposit (Orr, 1978).  Orr and Holmstead (1979, p. 
3-4) suggest potential explanations for past uranium deposition at Dieter Lake:
“The reason for the influx of U-rich material or preferred precipitation of the uranium at this 
point  in  the  depositional  history  of  the  basin  has  not  yet  been  determined,  however,  it  is 
probably the result of a number of factors including: 
1)  presence of an oxidation-reduction boundary 
2)  proximity of a delta-type environment 
3)  distribution of lows and highs in the basin floor 
4)  fluctuating sea level 
5)  influx of uranium-rich material into the basin.”
UEM  continued  to  delineate  the  distinct  characteristics  of  the  Dieter  Lake  Deposit 
(stratabound,  syngenetic,  uranium  horizons)  and  determined  a  genetic  model  for  the  type  of 
mineralization (Holmstead, 1981; p. 2):
1)  “Tectonic activity created an irregular basin in the Archean basement rock. 
2)  Subsequent  weathering  until  Aphebian  times  caused  partial  peneplanation  of  the
area and topographic lows were filled with conglomeratic material.
3)  Flooding  of  the  area  in  Aphebian  times  started  the  deposition  of  pelitic  material  in 
deeper parts of the basin.  An oxidation-reduction boundary formed, controlled by the 
depth of the water.
4)  Further  tectonic  activity  released  freshly  exposed  uranium  into  solution  as  the  U+6
species.
5)  When  waters  carrying  the  U+6  species  contacted  the  oxidation-reduction  boundary, 
U+6  was  reduced  and  uranium  precipitated.    The  adsorption  of  uranium  onto  clay 
minerals was probably a major factor for uranium fixation.
6)  A  prograding  delta  deposited  coarser  sediments  over  the  uranium  rich  horizon  to
protect it from erosion and leaching.”
7.   MINERALIZATION
Uranium  mineralization  at  Dieter  Lake  appears  syngenetic  stratabound,  limited  to  specific 
stratigraphic horizons or facies equivalents.  Disseminated uranium mineralization exists within a 
shale-wacke  horizon  of  the  Lower  Sakami  Formation.    The  horizon  was  initially  determined 
through  geophysics  and  surface  studies,  such  as  scintillometer  surveys  and  geochemical 
sampling.  Uranerz Exploration and Mining Ltd. (UEM) confirmed the presence of stratabound 
uranium mineralization in their 1978 diamond drilling program, indicating the strike length may 
be  in  the  order  of  6  km  (Orr,  1979b).    Uranium  values  were  obtained  from  each  drill  hole, 
supporting the possibility of a large mineralized area.  Mineralization thicknesses obtained from 
drill  holes  exhibited  thickness  variations.    Thicknesses  determined  by  radiometric  downhole 
logs, with a cutoff of 200 ppm U3O8, vary from 0.15 m to a maximum of 2.35 m, with an average
of 0.74 m.  Those determined by chemical assays with the same cutoff vary from 0.40 m to 3.00 
m, with an average of 1.04 m (Petura and Plouffe, 1981).
13
Low grade uranium mineralization at the Dieter Lake Property exists in oxide phase in the 
form of very fine grained (2.5 µ), low-temperature, sooty pitchblende.  The pitchblende occurs 
intergrown with the very fine grained carbonate-chlorite matrix of the shale, accompanied by the 
presence  of  goethite.    Carbonaceous  matter  commonly  constitutes  less  than  1%  of  the  rock 
constituents.    Mineralization  exhibits  a  preference  for  clay-rich  and  quartz-free  laminae  (Orr, 
1979c).    The  mineralized  horizon  is  well  compacted  and  recrystallized  with  a  high  fraction  of 
evaporitic precipitates, primarily carbonates, and elevated levels of phosphorous, vanadium and 
sulphides  (Dahlkamp,  2004a).    Transportation  by  ground  water  or  slowly  flowing  rivers,  and 
evaporitic  conditions,  caused  the  concentration  of  metal  ions  in  a  playa  occupying  the  Gayot 
Lake graben.  A reducing environment and bacterial activity at the lake bottom likely caused the 
precipitation of metals as either sulphides (Cu, Fe, Mo) or as oxides (U, V) (Gehrisch, 1987).  
Small  volumes,  generally  less  than  1%,  of  associated  minerals  include  pyrite,  bornite, 
chalcopyrite, chalcocite, molybdenite, galena and colloidal textured grains of manganese oxide.  
Trace element distribution maps, produced following the 1980 exploration program, show a very 
good correlation between U and Cu, as well as a good correlation between U and V, and U and 
Mo  (Gehrisch,  1981).    Molybdenum  mineralization  encountered  in  the  Lake  Vivian  areas 
appears to be associated with tectonized granite gneiss and biotite schist.  Specular hematite-
filled veinlets are common in sections containing MoS2 (Ore et al., 1980).
The wacke overlying the mineralized horizon was deposited within a lacustrine environment.  
In some areas, shallow, proximal, partly evaporitic environments are evident by reddish, coarse-
grained arkosic sediments with desiccation cracks and sodium-enriched strata containing more 
than  50%  albite.    More  distal  and  deeper  past  environments  consist  of  finer-grained,  mostly 
greenish  sediments  with  magnesium  enrichment.    The  matrix  of  the  wacke  unit  generally 
constitutes over 50% and consists of sericite, chlorite, chloritized biotite, and albite, with limonite 
and/or hematite (Dahlkamp, 2004a).  Generally the larger grains, 0.05 to 3 mm, are comprised 
of quartz, microcline, plagioclase, and minor granite and schist fragments.  About 40 to 60 cm 
above the mineralized ore horizon is a characteristic grey to black, coarse-crystalline carbonate 
bed.
The laminated mudstone unit, located just below the mineralized horizon, contains angular 
grains of quartz, albite, and minor potash feldspar, recrystallized carbonate grains and laths, and
14
minor, partially chloritized flakes of biotite running parallel to bedding (Dahlkamp, 2004a).  The 
matrix consists of albite, chlorite, sericite, and red iron hydroxides and hematite.
The basal conglomerate can be termed a sedimentary breccia.  About 10-50% of the unit 
consists of angular to subrounded grains of quartz, feldspar, and rock fragments.  The matrix is 
fine to medium-grained, inhomogeneous, and poorly sorted (Dahlkamp, 2004a).
The Sakami Formation rocks are virtually unmetamorphosed but have been diagenetically
compacted and recrystallized (Bélair, 1976; Dahlkamp, 2004a).
The  Archean  basement  rocks  at  the  pre-Sakami  paleosurface  exhibit  slight  alteration  by 
sericitization and chloritization with minor oxidation.  Mineralogical and petrological studies have 
indicated that some of the uranium mineralization is the result of xenolith inclusions, originating 
from the underlying granites of the Archean basement rocks (Orr, 1978).
The uranium mineralized horizon ranges in thickness, perhaps related to down slumping of 
unconsolidated mud during periods of fault activity and rises of sediment within the basin.  It is 
representative  of  past  lake  bottom  sediment,  located  as  a  transitional  facies  between  the 
laminated mudstone unit and the wacke unit.  Deposition was likely during a time of evaporation 
and  very  low  sedimentation  rates  near  the  redox  boundary  of  a  stratified  lake  (Dahlkamp, 
2004a).  The mineralized horizon is characterized by a change in texture from shale to wacke, a 
calcite-rich sequence, and disturbed bedding features, such as slumping, load structures, and 
convolute bedding (Orr and Holmstead, 1980; Gehrisch, 1981).  It is commonly olive green to 
brown,  with  red  tinges  in  mineralized  sections.    Shrinkage  cracks  host  calcite  veinlets  and 
occasionally remobilized pitchblende (Gehrisch, 1981).  A characteristic horizon exists 40-60 cm 
above  the  mineralized  horizon,  a  dark-grey  to  black,  coarse-crystalline  carbonate  bed 
(Dahlkamp, 2004a).
Exploration by UEM identified four major zones of uranium mineralization within the Dieter 
Lake area, the Vivian Zone, Nancy I Zone, Nancy II Zone, and Bert’s Zone.   The Vivian Zone 
appears to be the highest grade zone in the property area, with anomalous grades up to 0.56% 
over 1 m.  The Nancy I and II zones were originally one; however, drilling discovered a south 
westerly barren ridge dividing the zone into two (Gehrisch, 1981).  Grades up to 0.34% U3O8 
over 1 m were encountered.  The Bert’s zone was discovered in UEM’s 1980 drilling program 
with grades up to 0.22% U3O8 over 1 m.  The Nancy zones and Bert’s zone are lower grade 
than those in the Vivian zone but they remain open to the southwest.
The mineralization encountered in drilling of the Bert’s zone is potentially the north eastern 
extremity of a larger and higher grade zone (Gehrisch, 1981).  The mineralized zones appear to
15
have a north easterly trend, parallel the prevailing fault system and are open to the southwest.  
UEM provided typical stratigraphic sequences for the Vivian Lake zone and Bert’s Lake zone 
following their 1978 drilling program, Tables 7.1 and 7.2 (Orr, 1979).UEM reported anomalous 
grades, like the high of 0.56 wt% U3O8 over 1 m (hole 99) in the Lake Vivian Zone, supporting 
the  possibility  of  isolated  zones  containing  above  average  uranium  grades  (Orr,  1978).  
Diagenetic processes are often responsible for uranium enrichment in stratabound deposits.
Potential  exists  for  structural  traps.    UEM  mapped  faults  that  may  have  provided 
mechanisms  of  transport  for  fluids  and  favourable  sites  for  the  deposition  of  concentrated 
minerals  (Ore,  1978);  however,  throughout  UEM’s  exploration  programs  no  conclusive 
relationship was determined between faults and concentrated uranium mineralization.  Although 
the variation in thickness of the uranium mineralized horizon may be related to down slumping of 
unconsolidated mud during periods of fault activity and rises of sediment within the basin.

8.
2005 Exploration
The field season began with the mobilization of the crew in late June, and ended with the 
last  of  the  crew  leaving  late  August.    Helicopter  services  were  contracted  to  HeliTransport  of 
Trois-Rivières, Québec from July 16 to 31, 2005.  Expediting services were provided by Jean-
Marie  Arseneault  of  Radisson  &  Val  d’Or,  Québec.    Field  crew  consisted  of  Clinton  Davis, 
Jocelyn Tanton, James Bradley, Albert Stoffers, Patrick Kluczny and Mathieu Dallaire.  Cooking 
duties were managed by Claire Roy. 
8.1
Camp Set Up
A camp consisting of temporary structures was established during the winter/spring of 2005.  
It consists of 4 sleeping shacks, a kitchen shack, a wash/dry shack, a core shack and an office 
shack.  Concurrent with camp establishment, fuel was ferried up by fixed wing aircraft for the 
summer work.
8.2
Regional Geologic and Structural Mapping
17
The Gayot Lake Basin was geologically and structurally mapped including the collection of 
36  hand  samples  from  outcrops  and  boulders  (Fig.  8.1).    The  majority  of  the  mapping  was 
helicopter supported.    Instruments used  included  two  Exploranium  GR-110G  portable  gamma 
ray scintillometers and an Exploranium GR-130 minispec surveying gamma ray spectrometer.  
Sample results are presented in Appendix 2.
Two areas were surveyed in the flagged grid using the GR-110 to test the effectiveness of 
this exploration method.  The first was approximately 22 line km in an area to the southwest of 
Dieter  Lake  and  two  1-km  lines  south  of  Bert’s  Lake.    Variations  can  be  seen  and  it  may  be 
advantageous to extend the coverage using this simple method.
The  structural  interpretation  of  the  basin  was  refined  as  a  result  of  the  summer  program.  
Geologic information was added to the area, specifically the two gabbroic dykes that cross cut 
the basin north-south.  From an economic view point, samples of the gabbro dykes did not have 
anomalous metal values despite the presence of disseminated sulphides. 
8.3
Sediment Sampling
A 200 m by 200 m grid was laid with 158.6 km of line.  Geochemical sampling consisted of 
834  sediment  samples  taken  approximately  every  200  m  (Fig.  8.2).    Sampling  criteria  is 
discussed in “Sampling Method and Approach” and results are in Appendix 2.  The scintillometer 
survey was also conducted, the results are shown on Fig. 8.3.
There  does  appear  to  be  a  weak  correlation  between  the  previously  outlined  mineralized 
zones and the variations in the sediment sample elements.  As was the case following the 1980 
exploration program (Gehrisch, 1981), there is a good correlation between U and Cu, U and V, 
as well as U and Mo.  There appears to be an anomalous area to the west of Dieter Lake, where 
there are elevated levels for most of the metals contoured. See contour figures for U, V, Th, Ni, 
Cu and Mo (Fig. 8.4-8.9).  Variations in the distribution of individual elements may be partially 
explained by the nature of the underlying sedimentary rocks.   
8.4
Core
Core racks at Dieter and Ranger lakes from previous drilling programs were various states of 
disrepair.    Rack  rehabilitation  and  core  box  organization  are  discussed  in  “Data  Verification”.  
Holes for which no old log was available were relogged, as were some other holes to verify old 
logs.  All  available  core  was  reviewed  with  a  scintillometer,  resulting  in  the  collection  of  204 
samples  (Appendix  2).    Sampling  criteria  is  discussed  in  “Sampling  Method  and  Approach”.
18
Travel  between  Dieter  and  Ranger  lakes  was  done  by  quad  on  an  old  trail  connecting  the 
camps.  Core was split using hydraulic core splitters.
Results  of  the  core  logging  and  sampling  tend  to  verify  the  results  of  the  previous  work.  
During the original drill program more core samples were collected, but it is believed that there 
were sufficient check samples collected to verify the historic data (Appendix 3).   New data was 
incorporated  into  the  database  and  an  updated  resource  model  was  developed,  see  section 
14.2. 
8.5
Airborne Geophysics
Between  18  and  25  July  2005,  Fugro  Airborne  Surveys  conducted  a  GEOTEM® 
electromagnetic and magnetic survey of the Dieter Lake area on behalf of Strathmore Minerals 
Corporation.    Magnetometer  used  was  a  Scintrex  Cs-2  single  cell  cesium  vapour,  towed-bird 
installation  and  the  EM  system  was  GEOTEM®  20  channel  Multicoil  System.    A  total  of  29 
traverse lines were flown ranging in length from 8 km to 43 km, with a spacing of 400m between 
lines, and 11 tie lines were flown with a spacing of 4000m between tie-lines totalling 977 km for 
the complete survey. Two originally planned traverse lines were not flown.
Magnetic  data  was  useful  in  outlining  structures  in  and  around  the  property.    It  correlated 
well with structural and geologic mapping (Fig. 8.10).  The electromagnetic also outlined several 
structures, some that were not evident on the magnetic survey (Fig. 8.11).  However, it did not 
result in a clear picture of deeper levels of the basin as it was impeded by a resistive layer, likely 
a shaley unit.
9.
SAMPLING METHOD AND APPROACH
Sample  types  collected  on  the  Dieter  Lake  Project  during  the  2005  exploration  program 
included  sediment  samples,  grab  samples  and  core  samples.    This  work  was  planned  and 
carried out by Dahrouge Geological Consulting Ltd personnel.
For  the  sediment  sampling,  a  200  m  by  200  m  grid  was  laid  with  158.6  km  of  line.  
Geochemical sampling consisted of 834 sediment samples taken approximately every 200 m. 
Sample collection was done by land (foot and quad) or helicopter supported.
During geologic and structural mapping, 36 hand samples were collected from outcrops and 
boulders.    Most  of  this  work  was  helicopter  supported.    Gamma  ray  meters  used  were  2 
Exploranium  GR-110G  portable  gamma  ray  scintillometers  and  an  Exploranium  GR-130
minispec  surveying  gamma  ray  spectrometer.    All  of  the  samples  collected  on  the  project 
property were for uranium.
19
Based on scintillometer readings, 204 samples were collected from the historic core to verify 
old  data.  Gamma  ray  meters  used  were  2  Exploranium  GR-110G  portable  gamma  ray 
scintillometers  and  as  needed  an  Exploranium  GR-130  minispec  surveying  gamma  ray 
spectrometer.  Sampling criteria was based on notable increases in scintillometer readings over 
a  significant  length.  Background  readings  were  approximately  80  to  100  counts  per  second.  
Minimum sampling criteria were generally on the order of an average 150 counts per second 
over a minimum length of 30 cm (~1 foot) if there was a peak reading of at least approximately 
200 counts per second.
Historic sampling has been discussed in a previous technical report, (McCrea, 2005).
10.  SAMPLE PREPARATION, ANALYSES AND SECURITY
The sediment samples were processed by the Saskatchewan Research Council using ICP 
(partial & total digestion) and fluorimetry ICP where appropriate (Appendix 2).  The packages 
used were industry standards developed by Cogema and Cameco.
The grab and core samples were also processed by the Saskatchewan Research Council 
using ICP partial and total digestion and fluorimetry ICP where appropriate (Appendix 2).   The 
packages used were industry standards developed by Cogema and Cameco.
Samples were packed in 5 gallon pails, which were secured with packing tape and shrink 
wrap.  Samples were flown out of camp by J.-M. Arseneault and were secured in his hangar in 
LaGrande,  Quebec.    From  LaGrande  the  samples  were  shipped  to  Saskatchewan  by  Kepa 
Transport.
11.  DATA VERIFICATION
Areas of interest from as determined from historic data were re-visited, with the collection of 
sediment  and/or  grab  samples.    Sample  analyses  protocols  used  included  blank  samples, 
standards and repeats analyses.  Where necessary based on anomalous results, samples were 
asked to be re-analyzed.
A core dump was found at each of Dieter Lake and Ranger Lake.  The core was originally 
stored in core racks, which were in various states of disrepair.  Core racks were rehabilitated 
and salvaged where possible; otherwise core boxes were neatly cross stacked off the ground.
20
Core boxes were organised with minimal loss.  The original box tags were punched aluminium 
and most were still affixed to the boxes.  Fallen tags were collected and matched up to staple 
holes  and  partial  tags  when  possible.    Ordinal  boxes  were  also  matched  up  by  the  core  as 
needed.    Some  mineralized  intervals  were  stored  away  from  the  main  racks.    The  missing 
intervals from the Dieter Lake dump were found, but those from the Ranger Lake dump were 
not.    Despite  this,  there  were  mineralized  intervals  already  in  the  racks.    Most  of  the  historic 
sampling consisted of half core samples.  In the available boxes, there were few intervals that 
were  completely  sampled.    Core  samples  were  collected  by  2005  personnel  under  the 
supervision of Clinton Davis and were used to verify the available historic assays results.
12.  ADJACENT PROPERTIES
Some  of  the  information  and  geological  knowledge  regarding  the  Dieter  Lake  Property  is 
extracted  from  past  reports,  which  involved  an  area  larger  than  the  present  day  property. 
Mineralization  encountered  on  the  adjacent  properties  is  not  necessarily  indicative  of  the 
mineralization on the Dieter Lake Property and an attempt has been made to clearly indicate 
that any mineralization presented herein are site specific within the confines of the Dieter Lake 
Property 
13.  MINERAL PROCESSING & METALLURGICAL TESTING
In  April  of  1980,  a  bulk  trench  sample  was  collected  within  the  Lake  Vivian  showing  for 
radiometric  ore  sorting  and  metallurgical  testing.    Approximately  1618  pounds  (8  –  45  gallon 
drums) of uranium-bearing sample material was sent to Lakefield Research of Canada Ltd. for 
preparation and Ore Sorters Canada Ltd. for sorting.
Results indicated a good separation of ore and waste with sorting, while achieving a uranium 
recovery of about 97%; however, leaching was found to be difficult due to very fine grain size 
and  high  carbonate  content  (Holmstead,  1983).    Acid  leaching  (210.5  kg  sulphuric  acid  per 
tonne;  210  hours;  92%  recovery)  had  a  high  acid  consumption  but  required  shorter  retention 
times for greater recovery than alkaline leaching (168 hours; 66.7% recovery) (Gehrisch, 1981; 
Holmstead and Orr, 1981).  Increased fine-grained material decreases the average grade but 
increases the recoverable uranium content.  Metallurgical tests determined much of the uranium 
is likely present as adsorptions on clay minerals rather than more easily leachable pitchblende 
grains.  Due to radiometric ore sorting, or dilution, which doesn’t account for fine material, the
average grade of U3O8 (200 ppm cutoff) in the Dieter Lake Property area is 0.11% with a metal 
content of about 8600 tonnes (Petura and Plouffe, 1981).
21

14.2  Updated Estimates
Resource modelling was updated as part of the evaluation of the data collected during the 
2005 exploration program and compared to the historic resource model (Fig’s 14.1-14.3, Table 
4.4  and  Appendix  5).    Michael  Guo  used  ArcView  software  to  create  polygons  using  the 
Thiessen  (Voronoi)  method.    The  new  models  were  plotted  using  the  2005  core  sampling 
results, coupled with available historic interval assay data. Data constraints used were 200 ppm, 
500 ppm, and 1000ppm U3O8 over a minimum of 1 m thickness.  Polygons created had radii of 
200 m.    A  rock  density  of  2.67g/cm3  was  used  (Petura  &  Plouffe,  Feb  81).    Core  sampling 
methodology was presented in Section 8.4 for samples collected in 2005 under the supervision 
of Clinton Davis.
The combined historic and 2005 sample data for 96 holes, resulted in an average grade of 
0.057 % U3O8, based on a 200-m radius.  The Dieter Lake Property is considered to have an 
inferred resource of 24,424,306 lbs U3O8 (Table 14.4).
When  higher  cutoff  grades  of  500  ppm  and  1000  ppm  are  utilized,  the  resultant  average
grade increases to 0.108% U3O8, and 0.159% U3O8.

15.  OTHER RELEVANT DATA AND INFORMATION
23
15.1  Outstanding Issues
To the author’s knowledge, there is currently no known environmental, permitting, legal, title 
taxation, socio-economic or political issues that adversely affect the property.  No settlements or 
commercial  land  exploitation  exist  in  or  near  the  property  area.    Refuse  from  previous 
exploration programs prior to Strathmore may be an issue. 
15.2  Mining and Infrastructure
Presently, the property is only accessible by aircraft.  There is currently no infrastructure on, 
or in the immediate area of, the property.  Mining methods would depend on the geology of the 
area determined by exploration.  Estimated depths of the stratabound ore horizon, obtained from 
past  exploration,  are  from  about  50  m  to  more  than  400  m  below  the  surface.    Due  to  the 
depths, mining would likely be a combination of surface and underground methods.
16.
INTERPRETATIONS AND CONCLUSIONS
The  fall  2004  site  visit  and  summer  2005  exploration  confirmed  the  grade  and  tenure  of 
mineralization present at the property.  The 2004 work concluded that samples of mineralized 
boulders  from  near  Ranger  Lake  indicate  potential  for  higher-grade  remobilized  uranium 
mineralization.  The sericite, illite and chlorite from core samples, may be representative of an 
alteration halo associated with secondary mineralization related to the known mineralization.
The summer 2005 sediment sampling appears to outline mineralized zones, weakly for the 
individual zones, and moderately to strongly for the combined zones. The regional geologic and 
structural mapping helped to define the potential geometry of the mineralized zones.  This will 
help with targeting drill holes.  It also refined the known geology of the basin, for example the 
north-south trending gabbroic dykes.
The 2005 airborne geophysical survey found that a resistive layer impeded a clearer view of 
the  deeper  sections  of  the  basin  by  electromagnetic  methods.    Despite  this,  it  refined  the 
structural interpretation of the basin.  A large conductor was outlined in the north central portion 
of the basin.
The 2005 core sampling tends to verify the previously collected data.  In some instances the 
results of the resource modelling of the 2005 data show lower total amount of U3O8.  This can be 
explained in part by missing intervals in the core racks, or where some sections no core was left 
to sample.  When comparisons are made for intervals for which historic data is available, the
new  sampling  does  tend  to  verify  the  historic  results.  The  resource  modelling  also  tends  to 
confirm the geometry previously determined patterns of mineralization.
24
17.   RECOMMENDATIONS
UEM provided some recommendations following their summarization of the property data.  
UEM  suggested  increasing  the  drilling  density  to  fill  in  gaps,  running  regular  check  assays, 
producing a detailed density summary, obtaining more carbonate assays, and recognizing the 
presence of other milling elements (Petura and Plouffe, 1981).  These suggestions may aid in 
producing more accurate reserve estimations and will be taken into account in future exploration 
programs.
The  large  conductor  outlined  in  the  north  central  basin  from  2005  GEOTEM®  airborne 
survey warrants further investigation.  Areas with anomalous soil and grab samples should be 
followed up with denser sampling.
A diamond drilling program consisting of approximately 5000 m in a minimum of 10 to 12 
holes  should  be  completed  during  2007,  in  and  around  the  known  mineralized  areas  
(Appendix 1).
Potential liabilities and responsibilities associated with refuse and waster materials left from
previous exploration programs prior to Strathmore’s involvement need to be assessed.

The method of resource calculation is based on the polygon
method. This method creates polygons by bisecting each connecting 
line segment of drill hole perpendicularly. For the polygons at the 
edge, 200m radius arc is used to close the polygons.
Only those polygons which the ore bed thickness >=1m and the 
U3O8 > = 200ppm are used to calculate the resources.
The data used are combined from historic data and 2005 data. 
For the data with interval less than 1 meter, they are diluted to 1 
meter, and the interval increases in both directions evenly.
The rules for choosing the data for the calculation are:
 1)if the same interval has both historic data and new data, the 
new data are used for the resource calculation; 
2) the data with long intervals are used for the calculation if historic
 data and new data logged at a similar depth.
Coordinate Systym:
NAD 1983, UTM Zone 19N

The method of resource calculation is based on the polygon
method. This method creates polygons by bisecting each connecting 
line segment of drill hole perpendicularly. For the polygons at the 
edge, 200m radius arc is used to close the polygons.
Only those polygons which the ore bed thickness >=1m and the 
U3O8 > = 500ppm are used to calculate the resources.
The data used are combined from historic data and 2005 data. 
For the data with interval less than 1 meter, they are diluted to 1 
meter, and the interval increases in both directions evenly.
The rules for choosing the data for the calculation are:
 1)if the same interval has both historic data and new data, the 
new data are used for the resource calculation; 
2) the data with long intervals are used for the calculation if historic
 data and new data logged at a similar depth.

The method of resource calculation is based on the polygon
method. This method creates polygons by bisecting each connecting 
line segment of drill hole perpendicularly. For the polygons at the 
edge, 200m radius arc is used to close the polygons.
Only those polygons which the ore bed thickness >=1m and the 
U3O8 > = 1000ppm are used to calculate the resources.
The data used are combined from historic data and 2005 data. 
For the data with interval less than 1 meter, they are diluted to 1 
meter, and the interval increases in both directions evenly.
The rules for choosing the data for the calculation are:
 1)if the same interval has both historic data and new data, the 
new data are used for the resource calculation; 
2) the data with long intervals are used for the calculation if historic
 data and new data logged at a similar depth.

APPENDIX 1: DIETER LAKE PROPERTY
ESTIMATED COSTS FOR THE PHASE 2: DRILLING PROGRAM
Note:  Follow-up drilling program to Phase 1B Exploration (Summer 05); Project consists of 5000-m of diamond drilling in 15-20 holes.
Estimated time for completion is 60 days; with an additional 5 days contingency and an additional 5 days post-drilling
for the geological crew.  For this project approximately 40 days of helicopter support is required.
Cost savings can be made by ground mobilization during the winter if conditions are acceptable.
Expected time - Mar/Apr 07

Total Digestion:  A 0.125 g pulp is gently heated in a mixture of HF/HNO3/HClO4 until dry and the residue is dissolved in dilute HNO3.
The standard is CG515.
